# Durable Memory

## Cangjie Development Notes

### How To Write Cangjie Well
- Define data structures and function signatures before writing control flow. In Cangjie, clean type boundaries reduce later churn much more than clever implementation.
- Use `Option<T>` for real absence. Do not blur `None` with empty strings, empty arrays, or sentinel values.
- Keep functions small and single-purpose. Split parsing, normalization, validation, rendering, and summarization into separate helpers.
- Be conservative with collection pipelines. Prefer readable multi-step `ArrayList`/`HashMap` transformations over compressed one-pass logic.
- Treat strings, paths, JSON payloads, and command arguments as structured data. Avoid ad hoc concatenation when a helper or builder can keep boundaries explicit.
- Parse external input defensively. CLI args, env vars, settings, and file contents should always be trimmed, validated, and given stable defaults.
- Build frequently with `cjpm build -i`. Small compile-verify cycles are the safest way to work in a large Cangjie codebase.

### Common Cangjie Project Pitfalls
- Confusing “no value” with “empty value” creates fragile APIs. Use `Option<T>` early instead of patching null-like behavior later.
- Loose parameter design causes long-term churn. Decide positional vs named parameters, defaults, and return shapes before the function grows.
- Picking the wrong container type leads to awkward later code. Use `Array`, `ArrayList`, `HashMap`, and `HashSet` according to their actual role.
- Letting one function accumulate parsing, branching, formatting, and mutation makes Cangjie code hard to maintain. Split large flows aggressively.
- Hand-building strings and paths is a recurring source of bugs. Centralize path helpers, command-arg construction, and JSON shaping.
- Assuming numeric parsing always succeeds is unsafe. Every external numeric/config value needs fallback behavior.
- Spreading state across unrelated modules leads to dependency tangles. Keep clear layers for config, paths, runtime, and provider/backend logic.
- Printing errors deep in the stack without structured propagation makes debugging harder. Prefer structured returns or unified boundary-level rendering.
- Writing a lot before compiling increases debugging cost sharply. Small edits plus frequent builds are more effective than large unverified batches.
- Failing to extract neutral shared layers eventually causes circular dependencies. Introduce middle layers like paths, state, runtime, and provider interfaces early.
